STYLE & FASHION
1. Because . . .
fashion
is elevating
the L.L.
Bean look.
---
Want to upgrade your hiking
boots and flannel shirts?
Designers are cooperating
----
By Jason Chen
It has been more than four decades since Georgia-Pacific introduced the plaid-flannel-clad Brawny man,
enough time for us to witness the birth (and eventual death) of the square-shouldered and suited-up Master
of the Universe, the gelled metrosexual and the khaki-sporting normcore acolyte. And yet.
Somehow the idea of the rugged outdoorsman -- in his checked shirt, woolen trousers and hiking boots -- still
holds cultural sway. A number of designers this season, from Michael Kors to Bottega Veneta creative
director Tomas Maier to prince of street wear Alexander Wang, showed clothes that wouldn't look out of place
in an L.L. Bean catalog.
Currently, on high-end menswear e-commerce site Mr Porter, you can find a subtly avant-garde loden-hued
parka ($2,295) and a sweater that cleverly splices together cable and waffle textures ($730) from Japanese
label Sacai. Bottega Veneta offers a supersoft, refined henley ($390) that would pair beautifully with its
slim-cut, elastic-waist trousers ($590) that are garment-dyed to achieve a kind of dappled indigo effect. Need
a shirt? Add in New York brand Battenwear's patch-pocketed cotton flannel button-up ($205), woven rather
stylishly from a mix of navy and black thread.
Of course, parkas, henleys and flannels are already familiar to the average American man. But these more
refined iterations are targeted at particularly style-conscious gents. "Everything that we wear now as men is
sort of elevated from what it was," said Dan Rookwood, U.S. editor for Mr Porter. "For example, 10 years ago
if a guy was working out, he'd be wearing an old Metallica T-shirt and shorts with holes. Now it's got to be
designer. That's sort of translated into every part of his wardrobe."
Some might find the woodsy weekend-kit upgrade a bit excessive, but there are advantages. While the
clothes you might score at Bean, Lands' End or Eddie Bauer have great technical functionality, they may not
feel as good (cashmere is nicer against the skin than scratchy wool), and these versions can go from hanging
out in the country to drinks in the city without missing a beat. (Plus, many of the fashion versions, like Michael
Kors's windproof and nearly waterproof parkas, are technically sound as well.)
And as fashion trends go, "it's just so wearable for any guy," said stylist Ashley Weston, who has dressed
actors Paul Dano and "The Night Of" star Riz Ahmed. "No one will feel alienated by this look." Moreover, the

--- Page 57 ---
Page 57 of 227 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
L.L. Bean aesthetic captures a spirit to which many men still aspire. "It's rugged and masculine," said Mr
Porter's Mr. Rookwood, "which is just easier for guys to get into than something more challenging and
high-fashion."
---
2. . . . it's officially
sweater
weather.
But this isn't just any pilly cardigan the moths have been at. Belgian designer Dries Van Noten has
reimagined the classic shawl-collar knit by crossbreeding it with a swinging smoking jacket. Unlike your
grandpa's itchy knits, this belted sweater is as plush as the hotel robe you nearly swiped during your summer
vacation. Pair it with dark trousers to complement its urbane paisley-like motif. $990, mrporter.com
-- Jacob Gallagher

3. . . . brown is of
the moment.
Once upon a time, men's watch faces were as predictable as clockwork: white, black, maybe blue if you were
an unhinged risk-taker. This fall, though, watch-makers are working on their tans. These brown-faced tickers
are a subtly sophisticated finishing touch on everything from a navy suit to flannel-and-jeans. Overseas
Watch, $19,900, Vacheron Constantin, 212-317-8964; Bulgari Bulgari Carbon Gold Watch, $2,800,
bulgari.com; Seamaster Planet Ocean, $21,000, Omega, 212-207-3333
-- J.G.

4. . . . this mutant has
really arrived.
For years now, "to sock or not to sock?" has been a pressing summertime style conundrum. Mercifully, the
drop in temperature makes getting dressed less fraught. But here's a new dilemma: To sock-shoe or not?
Nike and Adidas have rolled out some cooler-weather shoes with ankle-huggers built in. Whether you call
them sneaker-socks, or sock-sneakers, they are intriguing many stylish, cutting-edge guys as a comfortable
alternative to high-tops or boots. Tubular Doom Sneakers, $160, adidas.com

5. Because . . . boots offer
room for expression.
---
A showy shoe can woo lovers of maximalism -- a key fall trend -- but these
patterned ankle boots are a more practical way to embrace it
----
By Nancy MacDonnell
Come late September, one of the joys of summer -- artfully cut sandals that reveal polished toenails --
disappears. Fall calls for more somber leather footwear. But this season, designers are offering a
cold-weather corollary to bright nail lacquer that's just as decorative: playfully patterned ankle boots, a riposte
to what Jessie Randall, co-founder and creative director of New York-based shoe label Loeffler Randall,
described as "a sea of black booties."

--- Page 58 ---
Page 58 of 227 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
As Ms. Randall suggested, black ankle boots, particularly ones with chunky heels, have become a fashion
mainstay over the past few seasons. No surprise there -- they're stylish in a low-key, go-with-everything way
that all but guarantees mass appeal. Women's reliance on them, however, is giving way to restlessness, thus
the swing toward embellishment. The sidewalk-friendly heel remains, but the fun factor has gone way up.
Loeffler Randall's new collection includes boots made from suede embroidered with Ukrainian folk-inspired
motifs, while Brian Atwood begat a pair crafted from velvet printed with a peacock-feather motif that's rather
art deco. Saint Laurent is offering a rich botanical brocade, Altuzarra has embroidered flowering vines and
Marc Jacobs, a pixelated floral leather.
"I think what's going on at Gucci, with the mixed prints, the bohemian feeling, feels fresh," said Mr. Atwood. "It
makes people a little less afraid of having fun." Fittingly, the inspiration for his statement-making boots was
David Bowie.
After failing to nab a pair of brocade Valentino ankle boots last year, Amanda Ross, a New York stylist,
designer and founder of editorial and e-commerce website A Ross Girl, now has her eye on the Altuzarra
booties. For her, printed boots are analogous to jewelry, an easy add-on that can give an outfit depth and
whimsy. "Instead of buying jewelry and getting decorative around the face, you can get decorative around
your feet," she said. "I like things that add a bit of joie de vivre."
Davinia Wang, founder of the Manhattan luxury accessories shop Edon Manor, credits the exclusive nature of
designer prints for part of the trend's success. Unlike a logo, she said, prints like the metallic medallion on the
Dries Van Noten boots she's stocking for fall are recognized only by those in the know. Moreover, she added,
"It's more difficult for high-street brands to copy. You can't do a pattern on the cheap."
In fashion's current maximalist climate -- color! texture! pattern! all together now! -- these ankle boots are an
easy way to appear au courant without feeling like a fashion victim. Better yet, these boots are actually made
for walking.
Ms. Ross said she plans to wear her booties with Celine's khaki separates. Ms. Randall has primarily been
sporting her designs with a mid-length skirt ("very chic") or jeans, which will likely be the choice of most
ankle-boot devotees. "Everyone in my office wears jeans," she said. "We have a kind of uniform -- jeans and
something simple on top -- and then we're more expressive with our accessories. These are not a dress that
you're only going to wear once a month. You can wear them a few times a week. They're a good sturdy
bootie."
---
6. . . . an
interesting
coat is a
one-step
update.
The fashion stakes go up when fall arrives and we all experience pressure to look polished and au courant.
The easiest way to accomplish that is a coat -- hidden for months and now ready, like a capricious actress,
for its star turn. Valentino's generously appliqued and embroidered camel duster might gobble your entire
clothing budget (plus your impulse-travel budget and your useless knickknack budget), but its ability to get
you "dressed" and on-trend with fall's over-the-top decorated look in one fell swoop is priceless. $22,000,
Valentino, 212-355-5811

7. . . . you can
wear perfume
that doesn't
disappear
by lunch.
Light, green scents are ideal for July days. The only problem: an hour after the initial spritz, your perfume has
pulled a Houdini act. One advantage of autumn is that stronger scents with real staying power feel apropos.

--- Page 59 ---
Page 59 of 227 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
One of the best is Byredo's new offering, created for the Swedish company's 10th anniversary. It's not
cloyingly heavy, but notes of spicy pink pepper and balsam fir give it a pleasing presence. A fun twist: The
bottle is Byredo's signature design, but you can customize its label with any name you choose thanks to the
included pack of rub-on letters. Unnamed Perfume, $230, byredo.com
---
8. Because . . . you get to haul out
that (not so) old jean jacket.
Round about now, colors are changing, both on the molting oaks and -- in a way less likely to inspire moody
8th-grade poets -- on denim jackets. Unexpected versions, like Saint Laurent's murdered-out black trucker or
Nudie's cream variety, are stretching the style well beyond classic cornflower blue.
"When you make [a denim jacket] with a darker fabric or tonal stitching, it is something that can be easily
dressed up," said Andrew Chen, co-owner of 3sixteen, a New York-based denim company that produces a
jacket so profoundly navy it's practically black. The inky jacket is also notably sleek, so much so that Mr.
Chen has seen some customers wear it with dress slacks. Marlboro-Man musty, it is not.
At the other end of the color spectrum are lighter denim riffs from labels like Battenwear, which lend
themselves to many denim-on-denim combos. "I see [our cream jacket] with blue jeans or black jeans or gray
jeans," said Morgan Sundberg, design director of Nudie Jeans.
These days, even when a brand does cut a jacket in the traditional indigo hue, it tends to design it for
maximum distinction. Gucci's Led-Zeppelinish entry comes riveted and embroidered. Meanwhile, more subtle
details, like A.P.C.'s "hidden" on-seam pockets or Levi's burnished seams and slightly frayed collar, give a
design character that makes it stand out quietly from the crowd, said Jonny Ribeiro, 29, a Brooklyn-based
interior designer and denim jacket aficionado. "I love things that have wear and show proof of life," said Mr.
Ribeiro, who describes his well-loved jean jacket as his "second skin." Find your own this fall -- just don't
settle for pedestrian old blue.
-- Jacob Gallagher

9. Because
. . . sleeves
aren't
skimpy.
---
Chillier days are welcome
opportunities to cover
one's arms. And this season,
the humble sleeve is
a canvas for creativity
----
By Kari Molvar
After months of baring one's imperfectly toned arms in steamy weather, it's a relief to put on clothing with
actual sleeves. Still, however skilled they are at hiding flaws, sleeves are usually uneventful -- pragmatic
tubes of fabric that run from shoulder to wrist.
That changed this season. On many runways, in fact, sleeves were the stars. At Gucci, "leg o' mutton" styles,
puffed at the shoulders, slimmed fastidiously at the elbow. Marni's versions ballooned beautifully. At Lemaire,
equally muttonous sleeves were rendered in an Aran knit, while Proenza Schouler's ultralong and lean ones
elegantly obscured models' hands. The more expressive your sleeves, it seemed, the better.

--- Page 60 ---
Page 60 of 227 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Upping the style ante around your arms is a playfully peacockish move. "I think women want drama in their
wardrobes," said Australian designer Kym Ellery, who has made interesting sleeves a signature of her
namesake label. "I'm noticing through street-style [photographs], people wanting to wear a silhouette that's a
bit more fun."
That's not to say that all intriguing sleeves shout: "Take my picture!" The drama can be subtle. "I think
sculptural clothing is a way of expressing yourself without patterns and logos," said Claire Distenfeld, owner
of Fivestory, a New York boutique.
And unlike trends that require a targeted workout routine (a killer crop top needs abs to match) or reveal more
skin than you care to show (cutouts can be cruel), the sleeve movement is forgiving. Statement-making
sleeves "hold no judgment," said New York-based stylist Micaela Erlanger, who dresses actress Lupita
Nyong'o. "They look great on all body types, and there's something incredibly elegant about being covered
up."
There are two schools of thought on how to wear these dramatic silhouettes. You can go all out,
complementing the volume on top with volume on the bottom, in the form of wide-leg pants or flaring skirts --
albeit ones with particularly defined waists. Fivestory's Ms. Distenfeld favors that approach. "I find it flattering
and feminine," she said. Or you can try the opposite tack: contrasting the supersize effect by scaling down the
rest of your outfit. Ms. Ellery suggested slim pants or a midi-length pencil skirt: "It's nice to balance the
sleeves with something quiet."
Advanced fashion practitioners can experiment with layering. Ms. Distenfeld, for instance, suggested pulling a
camisole on over a lavishly sleeved blouse for a sophisticated work look. Alternatively, wearing a knit top with
extra-long sleeves under a slip dress makes an otherwise minimalist frock "a bit edgier and of-the-moment,"
said Ms. Ellery.
If you're pursuing a more-is-more strategy in silhouette or styling, stick to neutral, refined tones to avoid
looking clownish. Your pants should probably not have sparkles, advised Ms. Distenfeld, adding that it's best
to let the statement sleeve do the talking. "Every outfit should have some element that takes a stand," she
explained, "and then everything else should follow that."
With any avant-garde garment, there are practical matters to keep in mind. Cascading ruffles, elongated
hand-obscuring cuffs and poufy sleeves can pose a risk at dinner where you might unwittingly "scoop up half
your meal," said Ms. Distenfeld. Helpfully, Marni's XL sleeves for fall can be removed (though we're not sure
where you'd stash them during the entree). Ms. Ellery often designs her sleeves with a means to rein them in,
buttons or a drawstring. You can always slide on a cuff bracelet, said Ms. Erlanger, to "hold up the sleeve a
bit."
Or just wear your balloon-sleeve blouse repeatedly to become accustomed to it. "It depends on the skill of the
wearer," said Ms. Ellery, "if she's used to having something complex on her body." Until then? Skip the soup
-- and stick with champagne.
---

can have

stylish can also be quite snuggly. That is certainly the case with Brunello Cucinelli's lipstick-red topper -- knit
by hand in Mr. Cucinelli's storied factory in Solomeo, Italy. It's petal-meets-chevron motif is meant to be a

a couch. $3,845, Brunello Cucinelli, 212-334-1010
---

re-enrolling in college,

--- Page 61 ---
Page 61 of 227 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
neighborhood children, an overflow of your kids' schoolbooks. At a time when the proportions of handbags

12. . . . woolly tights
are surprisingly
splendid now.

EATING & DRINKING
13. Because . . . it's crunch time.
---
Nuts are fall's great underrated ingredient. These recipes show what wonders even a squirrel-size portion will
do for a dish
----
By Kitty Greenwald
Shaved Kohlrabi With Radishes, Walnut Oil and Walnut-Mint Pesto
TOTAL TIME: 15 minutes
SERVES: 4
Preheat oven to 350 degrees. Spread 1 cup walnuts out in a small roasting dish. Toast in oven until aromatic
and deeply browned, about 5 minutes. Remove from oven and set aside to cool. // In a blender or food
processor, pulse half the toasted walnuts, 1 cup mint leaves, 3/4 cup grated Pecorino Romano, 1 cup parsley
leaves and 1 clove garlic, until finely chopped and well combined. With motor running, drizzle in 1/2 cup
walnut oil and 1/2 cup olive oil until oil is fully incorporated and pesto is smooth, 10-20 seconds. Season
pesto with salt and fresh lemon juice to taste. Set aside. // Use a mandolin or sharp knife to slice 2 small,
peeled kohlrabi into thin rounds. Season with a generous drizzle of walnut oil and a sprinkle of salt. Lay
kohlrabi slices flat on a serving platter, overlapping them slightly. In a bowl, toss 10 radishes, trimmed and
quartered, with a generous drizzle of olive oil, squeeze of fresh lemon juice and sprinkle of salt. Scatter
dressed radishes over kohlrabi. Dole out teaspoons of pesto all over the salad, peeling back layers of
overlapping vegetables to dot with pesto as needed. Roughly chop remaining walnuts. Garnish vegetables
with remaining walnuts and a pinch of salt. Serve with good bread, if desired.
-- Adapted from Renee Erickson of the Walrus and the Carpenter, Seattle

--- Page 62 ---
Page 62 of 227 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
---
Trout Amandine With Almond and Broccolini Puree
TOTAL TIME: 30 minutes
SERVES: 4
Bring a medium pot of salted water to a boil. Take 1 bunch broccolini, trim away dry ends and separate florets
from stems. Set stems aside for later use. Blanch florets until crisp-tender and bright green, about 2 minutes.
Transfer florets to a colander and rinse under cold water to cool. Pat dry and set aside. // Discard boiling
water, wipe pan clean and set pot back over medium heat. Add 1 tablespoon butter and broccolini stems and
their leaves, if attached, to pot. Saute until stems turn bright green, about 4 minutes. Add 2 tablespoons
sliced almonds, 1 cup whole milk, and a pinch each of salt and freshly ground white pepper. Bring to a
simmer and cook stems until very tender, 15-20 minutes. Add extra splashes of milk or water if pot looks dry.
Transfer everything to a blender and puree until smooth. If puree is too thick, blend in extra splashes of milk
until desired consistency is attained. Season with salt to taste. // Cook trout: set a large, non-stick pan over
medium-high heat. Add 1 tablespoon butter to pan, swirling to coat. Season 4 skin-on trout fillets (about 1 1/2
pounds total) on both sides with salt. Lay trout, skin-side-down, into pan. Cook until skins are seared, about 3
minutes. Add 2 tablespoons butter to pan. Once butter froths, spoon it over fillets, basting until meat firms up
and flakes when pressed, 3 minutes. Transfer fillets to a plate and set non-stick pan back over low heat. //
Add reserved blanched florets, 2 tablespoons sliced almonds, 2 tablespoons capers, drained, rinsed and
roughly chopped, 1/2 lemon, supremed, and juice of 1/2 lemon to non-stick pan. Saute, using a wooden
spoon to break up supremes, until aromatic, 1 minute. // To serve, lay each fillet on a plate, skin-side down.
Scoop broccoli florets, almonds, capers and lemons, along with remaining sauce from pan, over fish. Serve
with a good dollop of the broccolini puree under or alongside fish. Serve immediately.
-- Adapted from Daniel Boulud of Bar Boulud, New York
---
Fresh Tagliatelle With Lemon, Hazelnuts and Brown Butter
TOTAL TIME: 20 minutes
SERVES: 4
Set a large pot of salted water over high heat and bring to a boil. Meanwhile, in a large saute pan over
medium-high heat, toast 1/3 cup hazelnuts, shaking pan often, until aromatic, about 2 minutes. Transfer nuts
to a kitchen towel. Wrap nuts in towel and rub until skins flake off. Roughly chop nuts and set aside. // Wipe
saute pan clean and set over medium heat. Add 1 pound fresh tagliatelle to boiling water and cook until
noodles start to whiten and are just shy of al dente. Strain pasta, reserving 2 cups pasta water. Add 1 cup
reserved pasta water, pasta and 6 tablespoons butter, cut into 1-inch cubes, to saute pan. Toss until butter
melts and emulsifies with water, about 30 seconds. Add finely grated zest of 1 lemon, a squeeze of lemon
juice, toasted hazelnuts, 2 sprigs thyme, freshly ground black pepper and 1 ounce grated pecorino. Cook,
tossing, until lemon aroma intensifies and cheese melts, 1 minute. Adjust seasoning and add splashes of
reserved pasta water as needed to stretch sauce until it coats all the pasta. // Meanwhile, in a separate small
pan, melt 3 tablespoons butter over medium-high heat until it smells nutty and turns golden brown, about 1
minute. // To serve, distribute pasta among plates and top with dollops of fresh ricotta (about 6 tablespoons
total). Season lightly with salt, then drizzle with browned butter. Serve immediately.
-- Adapted from Hillary Sterling of Vic's, New York

17. Because . . . holiday baking can
yield unexpected rewards.
---
From Rosh Hashanah to Thanksgiving, this is a season of excuses
to bake. A cast-iron skillet can deliver all kinds of festive sweets -- and
ingenious solutions for putting the leftovers to tasty use, too
If you follow the news, you're probably aware that we waste a lot of food in the U.S. (an estimated 133 billion
pounds each year). It wasn't until I started working on my new cookbook, "Stir, Sizzle, Bake" (Clarkson Potter,
Sept. 27), about baking in cast-iron skillets, that the issue hit home -- literally. Developing the recipes left me
with an ever mounting surplus of loaves and batches. My pragmatic resolution: Use the leftovers in other
cast-iron-baked treats. I called the experiment -- and the ensuing chapter -- Baking It Forward.
One highly adaptable example: the challah d'epices, which is the kind of thing you might dream up if you're a
Jew who celebrates Christmas. Pain d'epices is a sweet, spiced loaf -- gingerbread's French cousin. I created
a yeasty challah version and upped the spice factor with a fragrant glaze.
The glossy, mahogany outcome tastes like late December, but the honey within signals the approach of the
Rosh Hashanah holiday in October, ensuring, per Jewish tradition, a sweet future. I recommend you turn the
stale remnants into bostock, an almond-enhanced, frangipane-topped vehicle for stale bread that makes
breakfast a special occasion and sanctifies the afternoon tea ritual.
-- Charlotte Druckman
---
Challah d'Epices
ACTIVE TIME: 1 1/4 hours

--- Page 64 ---
Page 64 of 227 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
TOTAL TIME: 5 hours
MAKES: 1 challah loaf
For the dough:
3 1/4 cups unbleached bread flour, plus more for dusting
1/4 cup rye flour
1 1/4 teaspoons ground cinnamon
1 1/4 teaspoons ground ginger
1/4 teaspoon ground cardamom
1/4 teaspoon ground white pepper
2 star anise pods, ground
1/8 teaspoon freshly grated nutmeg
1/8 teaspoon ground cloves
3 tablespoons plus 1 teaspoon butter, plus softened butter for greasing
3/4 cup warm water (110 degrees)
1/3 cup buckwheat honey
2 large eggs, plus 3 egg yolks
1 tablespoon finely grated orange zest
2 teaspoons active dry yeast
2 teaspoons coarse salt
For the glaze:
4 tablespoons unsalted butter
1/4 cup buckwheat honey
2 tablespoons plus 2 teaspoons fresh orange juice
1 star anise pod
1/4 teaspoon ground cinnamon
1/4 teaspoon ground ginger
1. Make dough: In a large bowl, whisk together flours, cinnamon, ginger, cardamom, white pepper, star anise,
nutmeg and cloves.
2. Grease a separate large bowl with butter. In a small saucepan, melt 2 tablespoons butter and let it cool a
few minutes. Pour melted butter into greased bowl. Add flour mixture, warm water, honey, eggs, yolks,
orange zest, yeast and salt. Using a rubber spatula or wooden spoon, mix ingredients until they come
together into a dough. (As it gets harder to stir, you may want to work with your hands.)
3. Turn dough out onto a floured surface and set greased bowl aside for use in next step. Knead dough until
smooth and pliant, 10 minutes. Dough will be sticky when you start kneading, so dust more flour onto work
surface as needed. Once dough stops sticking to surface, it's ready.
4. In a small saucepan, melt 1 tablespoon butter over low heat and let it cool a few minutes. Place kneaded
dough back in greased bowl and brush with melted butter. Cover bowl with a kitchen towel or plastic wrap, set
in a warm place and let dough rise until it doubles in volume, 90 minutes.
5. Punch down dough, cover again and let rise until it doubles in size again, at least another 45 minutes.
Meanwhile, in a 10-inch cast-iron skillet, melt remaining butter over low heat. Remove from heat and brush
melted butter over bottom and sides of pan to coat.

--- Page 65 ---
Page 65 of 227 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
6. Turn dough out onto a lightly floured surface and, using a bench scraper or sharp knife, split it evenly into
halves. With your hands, roll one half into a thick, footlong rope. Do the same with the second half. Coil first
rope into a circle and place in center of buttered skillet. Then wrap second rope around first, tucking edges of
outer rope under inner coil to connect the 2 ropes.
7. Butter a piece of plastic wrap and cover dough pan. Let dough rise once more for 45 minutes.
8. Place a rack in lower third of oven and preheat to 375 degrees. Make glaze: In a small saucepan, combine
butter, honey, orange juice and star anise pod. Bring to a simmer over medium-low heat. Remove from heat
and stir in cinnamon and ginger. Let glaze cool in saucepan a few minutes, leaving star anise to steep. Brush
dough liberally with glaze (about 2 tablespoons), reserving the rest.
9. Bake bread until it is golden brown and sounds hollow when you knock on it, about 35 minutes. As soon as
challah comes out of oven, brush it with another liberal coating of the glaze (about 2 tablespoons). Leave
challah in skillet for a couple of minutes to set before transferring to a wire rack to cool for about 30 minutes.
You should be able to lift the challah out of the pan quite easily with a spatula. Reserve leftover glaze and any
leftover challah for making bostock.
Find a recipe for gingerbread challah bostock at wsj.com/food.

20. Because . . . vintners toil so we can tipple.
---
Harvest winery visits are a classic fall excursion -- but they don't reveal what really goes into making that
glass of Merlot
----
By Lettie Teague
Harvest season is here, the time of year when oenophiles everywhere pile into cars and head to wine country
for an up-close and personal look at their favorite wineries. While visitors may sample recent vintages, tour
the vineyards and perhaps even indulge in a little ceremonial grape stomping, they aren't likely to see much
of the real action. Nor are they likely to know about the problems winemakers often face as they work to get
that great glass of wine into consumers' hands.

--- Page 66 ---
Page 66 of 227 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Broken sorting tables, rain, hail, the flock of birds that ate half the year's grapes are just a few of the possible
harvest mishaps. Things can go remarkably well or catastrophically wrong, and winemakers don't always
know which one it will be. With harvest, uncertainty is a given.
One of the few certainties is that the grapes will be picked. Choosing the harvest date is the most important
decision a vintner makes every year, once he or she has determined that the fruit is ripe, with an ideal
balance between sugar and acidity. Everything follows from this single decision. If the grapes are underripe,
the wine might have a green note; if they're too ripe, it might be too high in alcohol. So critical is the call that
assistant winemakers have told me it's the biggest difference between their position and the head
winemaker's job.
Once a date has been selected, speed is critical. The actual harvest, which can last days or weeks, is a
full-court press to get the fruit off the vine and into bins as quickly and efficiently -- and with as much care --
as possible. Just-picked grapes are in a fragile state, and their juice can easily oxidize if the fruit is bruised or
damaged.
Although harvesting grapes with machines is faster and more efficient, many producers still choose to harvest
by hand, either because their vineyards are too small, too steep or too narrow, or because they believe a
human being, though slower, can do a better job of picking the best grapes.
Time of day is also key; grapes are often harvested late at night or very early in the morning to avoid high
daytime temperatures. In Napa, where harvest can begin as early as August, days can reach 100 degrees.
Picking grapes during the less-sweltering hours benefits not only the workers but also the grapes, as cooler
temperatures preserve acidity and help sugar levels stay stable.
Even in the best conditions, though, harvest is an "endurance test and the ultimate cross-fit program," said
Morgan Clendenen, winemaker and owner of Cold Heaven Cellars in Buellton, Calif. She marked her 20th
harvest this year. This may sound substantial, but in an irony that winemakers sometimes joke about, being in
the business for decades like Ms. Clendenen translates to actually producing wine only a couple dozen times.
Of course, winemakers are making critical decisions long before harvest, such as when and how to prune
vines, whether to irrigate and whether to perform a bit of canopy management, removing the green leaves
that cover the fruit to ensure that it gets sun or that air circulates through the vines to prevent rot.
It's not just the vines that are cleaned up; wineries require rigorous maintenance as well, especially at harvest
time. Carlo DeVito, co-owner of Hudson-Chatham Winery in Ghent, N.Y., said he devotes hours to "cleaning
equipment, cleaning containers and cleaning the crush pad" before harvest. Winemakers often say it takes a
lot of water to make wine. They like to say it takes a lot of beer, too.
Of course, all these preparations may be for naught if Mother Nature doesn't cooperate. "One cannot control
the weather on any level. One can only react to it," noted winemaker Rich Olsen-Harbich, of Bedell Cellars on
the North Fork of Long Island, N.Y. Another North Fork vintner, Charles Massoud, of Paumanok Vineyards,
put it this way: "We are in a partnership with Mother Nature, but she is the senior partner."
The next time you open a bottle of wine this fall you might want to raise a glass to all the people behind the
scenes who helped bring it to your table. Here's to a happy, healthy, hail-free harvest season!

2011 Shafer Napa Valley Merlot $50
Though the 2011 vintage in Napa Valley was one of the worst of the past decades, the Shafer team turned
out a fine Merlot. Bolstered by some Cabernet and Malbec, this lush, mouthfilling wine has notes of cedar,
spice and red fruit.
2012 Marc Deschamps Pouilly-Fume Les Champs de Cri $30
"Complicated" is never a good word when it comes to vintage descriptors, but that's exactly what 2012 proved

finish.
2013 Soter Vineyards North Valley Chardonnay $32
This vintage is "a good example of the skill, challenges and sacrifices necessary to make good wines in
difficult years," said winemaker Tony Soter. Aged in new and neutral oak, it's a lithe wine, judiciously
balanced between fruit and acid.

21. . . . football
season means
muchos nachos.
Gina Hamadey's cookbook "Buenos Nachos!" (Dovetail, September) will keep you crunching all the way to
Super Bowl Sunday. It includes this recipe for a spicy, ranch-dressing-dolloped paper-bag version contributed
by Kristen Kish, winner of Season 10 of Bravo's "Top Chef." Take it along whether you're headed to a
stadium or a 50-yard-line seat in front of the TV.
---
Nachos in a Bag
TOTAL TIME: 20 minutes
SERVES: 4
Heat a large skillet over medium-high heat. Add 1 pound ground beef to pan. Cook, stirring and breaking up
meat with a spoon, until well browned, 8-10 minutes. Add 1 teaspoon salt, 1 teaspoon chili powder, 1
teaspoon ground cumin, 1/2 teaspoon freshly ground black pepper, 1/2 teaspoon smoked paprika, 1/4
teaspoon cayenne pepper, 1/4 teaspoon ground coriander and 1/4 cup tomato paste. Cook, stirring, another
minute. Add 1/2 cup water, bring to a simmer and cook until meat has absorbed liquid, about 5 minutes more.
Off heat, stir in juice of 1 lime. Set aside. // Divide 10 ounces tortilla chips (from 1 medium bag) among 4
brown-paper lunch bags then top with equal portions meat mixture, 1 cup grated sharp cheddar cheese, 1/4
cup chopped red onions, 1/4 cup pickled jalapeno slices, 1/2 cup cooked corn kernels, 1/2 cup sliced black
olives, 1 large tomato, cored and diced, 1/2 cup sour cream and 1/4 cup bottled ranch dressing. // Close and
shake bags well to distribute ingredients.
-- Adapted from "Buenos Nachos!" by Gina Hamadey (Dovetail)

23. Because . . . you can go back to school
minus the homework.
---
An Oxford alum finds her pedigreed university even more inviting sans the angst of exams
----
By Kate Maxwell
It was exam season in Oxford, and cadres of students patrolled the High Street, a few still cramming notes,
and consequently dodging lampposts, as they neared the Examination Schools. Their attire was identical:
Every student wore full academic dress, or subfusc, as it is known here -- black suits and white bow ties on
the men; black skirts and velvet bows for women; everyone in gowns, mortarboards and a carnation
buttonhole.
I was once an 11th-hour note-crammer, and as I surveyed them through the window of the Old Bank Hotel,
between mouthfuls of breakfast kipper, I felt a mixture of deep-seated dread and relief that I'd never have to
wear a white carnation again. I'd started at Oxford University in the fall of 1996, when the wisteria was turning
gold as if determined to match the stone buildings. Twenty years later, I'd returned to take a nostalgic tour,
because you don't have to be a student to access the university's quadrangles and cloisters (check ox.ac.uk
for details). Had the digital age eradicated the old traditions, I wondered? Would there be an artisanal coffee
shop on every corner?
Oxford, 60 miles northwest of London, is the oldest English-speaking university in the world, a center for
teaching since the 11th century whose domes and spires have starred in everything from "The Italian Job" to
the Harry Potter franchise. The university is divided into 39 colleges, all of which are ranked on the Norrington
Table system according to the classes of degree their students achieve. My own Oxford history began when,
as a high-school student, I visited Norrington-topper Magdalen College, where Oscar Wilde and C.S. Lewis
studied, so it was there that I started my return tour. I remember sprawling, illegally, on Magdalen's
cloister-framed striped grass quad and imagining three glorious years of punting and high jinks, with a little
studying thrown in, not unlike the pursuits of the characters in Evelyn Waugh's "Brideshead Revisited." Two
decades after that first visit, Magdalen's 15th-century cloisters, decorated with coats of arms and gargoyles,
looked every bit as inspiring as I remembered, though deserted, the scholars no doubt scribing upstairs. Still,
I found a boisterous game of croquet unfurling on the lawn in front of the noble New Building (completed in
1740s), while a trio on a punt drifted down Holywell Mill Stream, which runs alongside the college.
In the end, I'd taken the less challenging option, and, rather than Magdalen, applied to read English Literature
at St Edmund Hall, which has mostly languished near the foot of the Norrington Table. Best known for its
rugby teams and drinking games, "Teddy Hall" was the alma mater of my Scottish grandfather, Hamish, who
studied forestry here in the late 1920s. When I told him, with great pride, that I'd been accepted to his college,
he recommended I go to Edinburgh University instead.
Soon after arriving this time around, I did something I'd never have dreamed of as a student: I went to the
daily Choral Evensong service at Christ Church Cathedral. Our modest congregation was ushered to pews
beneath a stone fan-vaulted ceiling. The all-male choir, in red gowns, gave an invigorating performance, as
they have most days for the last 500 years. Once they'd bellowed their last note, I went to sniff out more
familiar ground, starting on St. Giles at the Eagle & Child pub. The literary group The Inklings, who included
C.S. Lewis and J.R.R. Tolkien, had dubbed it the "Bird and Baby" and gathered there to discuss their
fantastical tales of wizards, hobbits and talking lions. The pub seemed to be keener on marketing than in my
student days, offering questionable Lewis- and Tolkien-inspired art for sale. I ordered a half-pint of Black
Sheep beer and discovered, via some casual eavesdropping, that the chat hadn't changed much in 20 years:
A group of students was discussing their night out at Park End nightclub and the best place to buy cheap
groceries. If these were the literary giants of the 21st century, they were keeping it to themselves.
I walked further north, through Jericho, a fashionable suburb of Victorian terraced cottages bordered by the
Oxford Canal. Should the international craft beer and coffee movements and associated hipster industries
have made their mark anywhere in Oxford, it would, I thought, be here. Indeed, Walton Street's shop fronts
had been gussied up, but the residents were the same. Even Raoul's cocktail bar, the kind of place you went
when the check for your student loan had cleared, had survived, albeit with a newly extensive drinks list that
would rival the early English catalog of the Bodleian, Oxford's gargantuan research library.
My student diet at Oxford had consisted mainly of just-add-water noodles and doner kebabs from Ahmed's
van on the High (Oxonians drop the "Street"). Things always looked up when my parents were in town,

--- Page 69 ---
Page 69 of 227 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
however. They'd book a table at Brasserie Blanc, Brown's or Gee's, all of which remain. My corner table for
one in Gee's olive-tree-and-twinkly-light-filled conservatory that evening felt most indulgent, and the food --
local asparagus and chargrilled guinea fowl -- tasted even better than I remembered. That's one thing that
has changed in Oxford. England's cuisine has ratcheted up in the last two decades, and what's cooked
between the dreaming spires has also improved no end -- even if the places it is served are the same.
The next day, I walked back through Jericho, across Port Meadow -- the grassy expanse sprinkled with
daisies and buttercups, where cows graze and from which you can spy the university church's spire -- to the
Perch pub. This 17th-century coaching inn across the Isis (the name given to this stretch of River Thames
eons before the advent of the terror group) has had a country-casual refurb and its food, as well as its bucolic
setting, is now well worth the half-hour walk from the town center.
Before I made my way back to London, I visited the gleaming new Weston Library, opened by Prince William,
where a copy of the Gutenberg Bible and Oscar Wilde's court summons are displayed, then took a last
nostalgic turn around the Radcliffe Camera, a science library and a strong contender for world's best-looking
building for books. As I raised my phone to snap a picture of the amber stone columns radiant in the evening
light, a cyclist in subfusc screeched and swerved to avoid me, yelling "Tourist!" Rather than lament my
outsider status I decided to celebrate it. Keep your carnation buttonhole and your mortarboard, I thought. The
Oxford I left is as well preserved as the Mummies in the Ashmolean Museum (which, incidentally, also has
decent food and views at its new rooftop restaurant), but the city has become a much better place to visit.

24. . . . the great migration
is greater later.
The pesky thing about so-called wonders of the world is that people want to see them; crowds are inevitable.
Case in point: the Great Wildlife Migration, when more than a million wildebeest and zebra make their annual
round-trip from Tanzania's Serengeti to the grasslands of Kenya's Masai Mara, across the crocodile-infested
Mara River. As far as bloody spectacles go, "Game of Thrones" has nothing on the animal kingdom. Though
the timing varies year to year, the migration typically kicks off in May, which allows a stampede of summer
vacationers to descend with their telephoto lenses. Nina Wennersten, of the travel company Hippo Creek
Safaris (hippocreeksafaris.com), counsels patience: "Wait until late September or October. That's when the
herds head back to the Serengeti, and there are fewer people around to spoil the views." As for where to
bunk down close to the action, the new luxe mobile camp Roving Bushtops, now in the central Serengeti,
does its own migrating. Come January or February, during the wildebeest birthing season, it will follow the
herds to the southern Serengeti. bushtopscamps.com
---
25. . . . election distraction
is available in D.C.
The National Museum of African American History & Culture, which opens Sept. 24 on Washington, D.C.'s
National Mall, has been in the works even longer than Boston's Big Dig. The original idea came up in the
early 1900s, and in the last decade the institution has been on a kind of multicontinent antiquing spree to
furnish its new home. A filigreed bronzed exterior, designed by architect David Adjaye, recalls the work of
enslaved New Orleans ironsmiths, and within, the nearly 40,000-piece-strong collection includes the
Mothership stage set for the Parliament-Funkadelic music collective, shards of a sunken slave ship and
handmade hats by early Philadelphia milliner Mae Reeves (reproductions are for sale in the shop). It's an
expansive story; if it weren't in the nation's tower-adverse capital, it would easily fill a skyscraper.
nmaahc.si.edu
-- Ryan Haase

Big Easy -- New Orleans -- is the home dock for the 150-passenger Louisiane. And "easy" seems to be the
guiding spirit for its five- to 10-day nearly all-inclusive sailings on the Mississippi and Ohio rivers. While the

--- Page 70 ---
Page 70 of 227 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
lower Mississippi owns bragging rights to culture -- the blues belong to Memphis; jazz to New Orleans -- it's
the upper river that holds a monopoly on the bluffs and the beauty. "The river below St. Louis has been
described time and again, and it is the least interesting part." That observer, of course, was Mark Twain, and
surely there is no more stylish way to visit his Hannibal, Mo., home than onboard a white-frosted riverboat.

27. Because . . . New Orleans takes
Halloween dead seriously.
---
If you want a haunting autumn getaway, this city buries all others
----
By Steve Garbarino
Few cities revel in the macabre and dearly departed as New Orleans does. A slew of haunted houses,
vampire tours and ghost-themed excursions run year-round, and a popular bumper sticker boasts a rare skill:
"New Orleans: We Put the FUN in Funeral." So it's no surprise that locals excel at Halloween-specific
festivities, which begin soon after September's lethargy-inducing humidity lifts; by mid-October, the city is
back in party mode. And this year, thanks to new restaurant and hotel openings, New Orleans is even more
equipped to bring weary visitors back from the dead.
The two biggest annual Halloween events are the Krewe of Boo parade (Oct. 22) and Voodoo Music + Arts
Experience festival (Oct. 28-30). Warning: Both are maddeningly popular. Find a spot early. At the parade,
which rolls through the French Quarter, with all its hidden gardens and Anne Rice-inspiring alleyways,
participants with skeleton face-paint and monster-themed floats rival Mexico City's Day of the Dead for eerie
eye-candy.
A week later, in New Orleans's City Park, multiple stages will draw 100,000 or more concertgoers to the
three-night Voodoo fest. Set amid stone bridges and 600-year-old live oaks, the concert attracts marquee
names like this year's headliners, Arcade Fire, the Weeknd and Tool. After the show, cross a bayou
footbridge and peek inside Esplanade Ridge's St. Louis Cemetery No. 3, one of the creepiest in the city.
Even the tony Garden District rises to the All Hallows occasion, when the stately antebellum and Greek
Revival manors on St. Charles Avenue are spookily transformed -- their facades and wrought-iron work
swathed with cobwebs and giant spider props almost the size of the "Alien" that inconvenienced Sigourney
Weaver in the 1979 film. Ambitiously landscaped grounds are dotted with tombstones, crawling with
skeletons and lurching mechanical zombies. Witches on brooms swing from oaks, magnolias and banana
palms.
Daylight hours can prove gleefully menacing too. Gussy up to enter the turreted Victorian sprawl that is the
Commander's Palace restaurant for its boisterous Haute Creole jazz brunch and 25-cent Martinis, then walk it
off in the decrepit Lafayette Cemetery No. 1 across the street, peeking into crumbling mausoleums and
getting lost in its labyrinthine maze of tombs.
When night falls, visitors have a dizzying array of dining options. Since Hurricane Katrina, in 2005, nearly 700
restaurants have opened in the city. Many ushered in an adventurous foodie renaissance and established
themselves in once-blighted or storm-ravaged corridors where tourists previously seldom ventured.
Nearly 60 restaurants debuted this past year alone, including standouts such as Shaya, an Israeli restaurant
in the city's Uptown section, that nabbed this year's "Best New Restaurant" award from the James Beard
Foundation. Others worth seeking out: Seaworthy, an airy oyster and seafood bar situated in a historic
townhouse in the Warehouse District; Cavan, a seafood spot nestled in an old Victorian on Magazine Street;
and Primitivo, a smoking-and-grilling meat-lover's fantasy. Celebrated chefs such as Emeril Lagasse and
Bayona chef Susan Spicer are debuting new gustatory outposts too. All these newcomers are keeping the
iconic white-tablecloth French-Creole mainstays -- now dusted off -- on their toes, including power-lunch and
party-palace stronghold Galatoire's, the handsomely renovated Brennan's, and Tujague's, which turns 160
this year and houses one of the country's most colorful stand-up bars.

--- Page 71 ---
Page 71 of 227 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Speaking of watering holes, the walking dead can now find Corpse Reviver cocktails in hopping nightlife
neighborhoods like Mid-City, Broadmoor, St. Roch, Carrollton and the Central City district's Oretha Castle
Haley Boulevard.
The tipsy, the spooked or the lazy will soon find it easier to get from Point A to Point B with the city's new
streetcar line expected to be in operation later this fall. Meanwhile, stretches of new bicycle paths, unheard of
just a few years ago, now make the inaccessible a breeze. Rent a gear-free cruiser at Bicycle Michael's in the
Faubourg Marigny or Uptown at Mike the Bike Guy and catch all those architectural details and hideaway
bars you might miss from a car window.
And if you're feeling above it all, you can quite literally peer down on the debauchery from two romantic
rooftop lounge bars at notable new hotels: The Warehouse District's Ace Hotel New Orleans and the Garden
District's venerable Pontchartrain, reopened earlier this year after a massive renovation. You won't find a
Corpse Reviver on the latter's cocktail menu, but you will find a Death in the LGD (Lower Garden District). A
concoction of gin and sparkling wine, it's not as frightening as it sounds.
---
28. . . . hot springs and
cool air mix nicely.
Long before the swanky ski resorts and craft breweries arrived, Colorado was a place health-conscious
tourists mobbed, drawn to its dry mountain air and multitude of mineral springs. By the mid-1800s, spring-fed
sanitariums had sprung up all over the Rocky Mountains, so many that this summer the state designated a
720-mile historic hot-spring loop, encompassing eight thermal regions. The springs vary in size and facilities.
At the foot of Mount Princeton, in Nathrop, they're the centerpiece of a resort (with 140 rooms and a spa)
where you'll want to stay awhile. Strawberry Park Hot Springs, in Steamboat, merits a brisker day visit. But if
you feel like earning your salubrious soak this fall, consider one of the dozens of undeveloped, hike-in hot
springs, such as Rainbow Hot Springs, with its multiple natural pools and a steaming waterfall, hidden at the
end of a 5-mile trek into the Weminuche Wilderness. A much shorter but not effortless hike leads to Radium,
a cliff-side thermal pool on the Colorado River. It's just a mile-long trail, but it's a steep incline and then a
rocky descent to the pool.
-- Brigid Mander
---
29. . . . anyone can know
what kind of leaf
they're peeping.
Autumn road trippers can discover there's more to spot in the wild than errant Pokemon by downloading
iNaturalist. The California Academy of Sciences app encourages users to train their lenses on mysterious
flora and fauna. Upload photos of an unknown species to have it reviewed by a community that includes
botanists, birders and researchers. Response time varies -- a pair of birds we uploaded were identified within
an hour as House Sparrows; a fallen leaf was revealed as a Silver Maple within a day. Each finding fuels the
Academy's research. inaturalist.org
-- R.H.

30. Because . . . beaches are
distinctly better when empty.
---
Many of the most scenic island destinations in the U.S. and Europe
clear out in the fall. That's your cue to move in
----
By Chaney Kwak

--- Page 72 ---
Page 72 of 227 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Islands in summertime belong to swimmers, sun-worshippers and proponents of the #everybodyisbeautiful
movement. Islands in the fall belong to the romantics. You'll probably need to wear a sweater while you're
beachcombing, but the crowds have left and the waterfront hotels and restaurants are usually yours for the
taking. Our fave four:
1. Block Island, R.I.
This fall, America's first offshore wind farm will retire Block Island's diesel generators, making this Atlantic
isle, between Montauk and Martha's Vineyard, even more enviably pristine. Salt-pickled cottages and
Adirondack chairs look out on sea vistas that have changed little over the years, save for the five windmills. In
September and October, called "local summer," the 17 miles of beaches are practically devoid of visitors
other than the migratory falcons and hummingbirds that recharge here. Recharge yourself at the newly
renovated Hotel Manisses, steps from the ferry landing (from $299; hotelmanisses.com).
2. Orcas Island, Wash.
The largest of the San Juan Islands, Orcas may not have stoplights or chain stores but it does have serious
foodie cred. Island orchards, farms and shellfish purveyors supply a number of good restaurants such as
Hogstone's Wood Oven, owned by farmer-restauranteur Jay Blackinton, a James Beard Award semifinalist.
The Orcas Island Farm Tour, on Oct. 2, offers guided field trips. In between eating your way around the
island, you can hike through the cedar forests and even sleep in one -- a glamping outfit in Moran State Park
stays open until Oct. 9 (from $99; stayleanto.com).
3. Isle of Skye, Scotland
Even in the dead of fall on this eminently hikeable Hebrides isle, the translucent water off Claigan Coral
Beach brings to mind the Caribbean more than the Scottish Highlands, and the island's inlets, lochs and
oddball geological formations look even more dramatic when the trees turn to gold. Best of all, when the chill
sets in, find the closest Highland malt. As for accommodations, the Victorian cottages on the grounds of the
14th-century Dunvegan Castle have been spiffed up without losing their charm (from about $398;
dunvegancastle.com).
4. Sardinia, Italy
Once the yachts motor away and the ritzy resorts shutter for the season, this Mediterranean showoff returns
to its unassuming self. The biggest crowds you'll find are the locals weaving around the Autunno in Barbagia
festival, held each weekend from September through December. That's where you can stock up on regional
cheeses, liquor and other traditional foodstuffs (cuoredellasardegna.it). Fall also sees prices drop, making it
an affordable time to check out Costa Smeralda, celebrity central in summer. Book a room at Cervo Hotel,
overlooking the old harbor (from about $210 a night; hotelcervocostasmeralda.com).

$8,400 after Oct. 27 (elegant.ie).

DESIGN & DECORATING
33. Because . . . watching
paint dry is dull.
---
And it dries much faster in the fall. Five
wall-color trends to try at home
----
By Elizabeth Anne Hartman
It's time to address the cautious neutrality that has characterized most Americans' wall-color choices of late,
not only because interior design has taken a turn for the spirited, but because fall's low humidity and coolness
are ideal for working on indoor projects.
Cocooning
In his Sag Harbor home, New York designer Steven Gambrel created an enveloping den with tone-on-tone
purples: walls, moldings, even doors in Benjamin Moore's Passion Plum; a purple tufted linen sofa and
aubergine lampshades. "Dousing all the surfaces of a room with the same color lends quiet and calm," he
said. "Accent walls are for those who can't commit," said Adam Rolston, a partner at INC Architecture and
Design in New York. Allover, deeply saturated color -- from jewel tones such as Farrow & Ball's moody
Inchyra Blue to high-gloss black -- create a sanctuary. Even little rooms benefit. "No small, dark room ever
got bigger or better by being painted white," said Chicago designer Tom Stringer.
Overhead Shine
"Ceilings are often underutilized," said New York designer Phillip Thomas. "I turn to metallic paints to carry
and reflect light through a space." Like a pair of zebra Mary Janes with a staid gray suit, "contrast makes a
space interesting," added New York-based designer Kati Curtis, who recommends a shimmering ceiling next
to matte walls.
Global Pinking
"The right pink has warmth and depth," said Los Angeles designer Michael Smith. "It can be empowering."
The current trending version is not pale or cotton-candy pink but a gender-neutral shade dirtied with a touch
of blue or gray, like Farrow & Ball's Setting Plaster. Mr. Rolston pairs pink with beige, likening the scheme to
Botticelli's "Birth of Venus." "It's so perverse, so beautiful."
Fresco Fever
"Integral plaster is the new paint," said Mr. Rolston. Whether enlisting Venetian plaster, which contains
marble dust, or its Moroccan cousin, Tadelakt, which has lime and soap in the mix, designers are adding
depth and detail with age-old techniques. Anyone can achieve similar effects, without hiring expensive pros,
with materials such as Annie Sloan's chalk paints or Ralph Lauren's Polished Patina product. Brooklyn
designer Kathryn Scott turns to lime paint, such as the TransMineral USA line, "to embrace an old-world
aesthetic, with a very earthy, welcoming surface."
Black Details

--- Page 74 ---
Page 74 of 227 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
If colors such as oxblood and pink make you want to dive into a bucket of Decorator White and call it a day,
consider black trim against white walls, another au courant strategy. New York designer Benjamin Vandiver
uses the technique to create architectural interest where little exists. For a quick remodel of a New York
apartment, he sprayed casement window frames with Farrow & Ball's Railings No. 31 in satin finish, and that
alone "made a huge difference," he said.
Pick a rose: Contemporary pinks are a little dirty, not saccharine, said New York designer Adam Rolston.
Antique Coral 1198, $70 per gallon, benjaminmoore.com
Factor in finish: Shiny black is sexy and chic, said Houston designer Katie Scott, while matte is more somber.
Eurolux Gloss Black, $50 per liter, finepaintsofeurope.com
Rush for gold: "Metallics are great for reflecting light into dark spaces," said Mr. Rolston. Metallic Paint in
Champagne, $45 per quart, modernmasters.com

39. Because . . . you can
pick an apple tree.
---
While a gastronome picks apples in the fall,
a gardener dreams of planting the entire tree
----
By Michael Tortorello
Two desires -- to collect apples and plant a fruit tree -- coincide in fall. You sample apple varieties at the
same time that catalogs and nurseries take orders for trees to deliver in spring.
For a few years, I've had designs on installing a pair of heirloom apple trees outside my apartment in St. Paul,
Minn. (If I wanted to munch a Gala, I could buy it at Costco.) The Italianate mansion that houses our condo
was once an archbishop's residence, and I suspect the old lawn held orchard trees -- for the fruit and the
pale, perfumed blossoms in April, as well as the charismatic physiognomy of the limbs. The lower branches,
with their weathered gray bark and horizontal growth habit, remind me of an elephant's trunk. And of course,
an apple tree along a front walk is the ultimate vending machine, dispensing snacks every time you pass.
Modern-day critics -- that's code for my wife -- complain about apple trees: the leaf blights, the maggots, the
unpicked fruit that smashes on car hoods. I offer two remedies: organic sprays and an acceptance of
imperfection in the garden. If you can't embrace either, an apple tree probably isn't for you.
Selecting the right trees -- you'll probably need two varieties for successful pollination -- makes everything
easier, said horticulture writer Lee Reich, who has experimented with some 20 apple varieties at home in
New Paltz, N.Y. Order one- or two-year-old bare-root trees to start, he advised, basically 4-foot-long sticks
with a shock of roots that cost $25 to $30.

--- Page 76 ---
Page 76 of 227 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
You'd rather spray, prune and harvest "on a short ladder, or no ladder," said Mr. Reich. The solution is an
apple tree on a "dwarf" or "semi-dwarf" rootstock, which grows to 6 to 9 feet.
Gidon Coll, founder of Original Sin Hard Cider, planted 100 varieties on dwarf rootstock at his family's Hudson
Valley dairy farm. He suggested Rubinette, a fussy tree whose fruit's sweet-tart balance earns reviews as
"maybe the finest eating apple in Europe." An easier-to-grow option is St. Edmund's Russet, Mr. Coll said.
The sweet, not sugary, fruit is the yellow-brown of a 1970s cable-knit sweater, with a similarly rough skin.
I ordered the trees a couple of weeks ago. And, until spring, my work on the home orchard was done.

43. Because . . . you can sway
coolly at your campsite.

--- Page 77 ---
Page 77 of 227 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
---
Looking to spend a weekend in the woods without the burden of a cumbersome
tent? Consider the breezy ease of a camping hammock
----
By Brigid Mander
Autumn is, in many ways, the ideal season to go camping: Parks are no longer teeming. The bug population
is in decline. Still, even with the industrious mood that fall inspires, setting up a tent can be laborious. So why
not ditch it for an easier solution: a camping hammock?
If you doubt you can sleep comfortably suspended between two trees, fear not. Camping hammocks are
neither constructed nor suspended like the one swaying in your backyard; these use taut, technical fabrics
and are more stable, which means you're less likely to flip out of one. And to minimize your exposure to the
elements, you'll most likely string a bug net and rain tarp over you. (Don't worry: still easy.)
Sales of camping hammocks have doubled between 2013 and 2015, according to market-research firm NPD
Group. And with good reason: Unlike with a tent, you needn't exhaust yourself finding flat ground or clearing
an area of rocks. If the earth is damp, no problem -- you're above all that. And once you get the hang of it,
hammocks are straightforward to set up; no fiddly tentpoles here.
To keep the autumn chill off your back as you sleep, you can attach one of the fitted "underquilts" that most
companies offer -- an insulated sling that sits under the hammock. And of course your sleeping bag and
standard sleeping pad will provide extra structure and warmth.
In fact, if you're concerned about the cold, don't be. To test hammock camping in typical fall temperatures, I
headed to the mountains last month with the Hennessey hammock shown above. Although the temperature
dropped to a nippy 35 degrees at night, I was perfectly warm, thanks to the sleeping pad and down sleeping
bag, rated for 20 degrees, that I brought along. The rain tarp that came with the hammock effectively warded
off the wind.
To suspend your hammock, simply wrap "tree straps" around two appropriately spaced trunks. Because this
flat webbing is wider than rope, it won't damage the bark. And tempting though it may be, don't hang your
hammock more than a few feet off the ground. It will be easier to climb in and out if the hammock is lower,
and in the unlikely event of a suspension failure, you won't have as far to fall.
Below, four hammocks that will comfortably swaddle you into dreamland.
THE ENTRY MODEL
Eagles Nest Outfitters Single Nest Hammock
While you could just as easily string the Single Nest on the porch (it comes in 21 different colors, making it
easy to coordinate it with your outdoor furniture), it's designed to travel to less genteel environments. This
hammock weighs just 1 pound and can be bunched up into a softball-size bundle, but it supports up to 400
pounds. Keep in mind that the Single Nest offers less space than the others discussed here and will only
accommodate a skinny sleeping pad. $60, eaglesnestoutifttersinc.com
THE ALL-IN-ONE PACKAGE
Hennessy Ultralight Backpacker Asym Classic
Based on the design of World War II Army hammocks, this one-person swayer is all about utility. Mosquito
netting is sewn right in; there's a sleeve to hold your sleeping pad in place; a Velcro-sealed doorway allows
for easy entry; and an asymmetrical shape allows you to lie across the centerline for a flatter position. The
included rain tarp can be rolled up along with the hammock and stowed in the stuff pouch, making setup and
take-down especially fast and easy. $240, hennessyhammock.com
THE MCMANSION
Kammok Roo
Lightweight but massive (about 10 feet long by 5 1/2 feet wide), this hammock is an all-enveloping cocoon of
strong ripstop fabric. Although it's intended to accommodate two people, keep it all to yourself. It's wide
enough to allow solo sleepers to lie fairly flat and slightly across the centerline. Its sturdy construction made it
feel very stable, even if you're moving around. $99, kammok.com

--- Page 78 ---
Page 78 of 227 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
THE TENT ALTERNATIVE
Tentsile Stingray
A cross between a hammock and tent, this suspended sleeper consists of a relatively flat and firmsurface that
attaches to three (not two) trees. Its bug-net roof allows for stargazing, and the comparatively palatial interior
is made for a restful night's sleep. While easy to set up, you will have to find three trees growing the right
distance and angle from each other. Important tip: Familiarize yourself with how the ratchet straps work
before heading into the woods. $650, tentsile.com

46. Because . . . silent boating is a virtue.
---
Discover the quiet bliss of cruising across a lake with the help of an electric outboard motor
----
By Dan Neil
Ah, fall in the Ozarks, when a largemouth bass's thoughts lightly turn to eating littler fish. When the weather
turns cool, these bruisers come up from deeper water to feed on baitfish. And some of the biggest, the
lunkiest, live beyond the reach of gasoline.
"Around here you got a lot of little lakes where they don't allow gas-powered boats," said Dale Eberlin, owner
of Eberlin Boats & Motors, in Hermann, Mo. "A lot of them are lakes in the middle of subdivisions," he said.
"There's nowhere for the water to go, so you can't have a bunch of gas engines puking in the water."
This year, Mr. Eberlin, whose father began the business in 1955, became a dealer for Torqeedo, a German
manufacturer of electric and hybrid drives for maritime applications. Only a handful of companies make

--- Page 79 ---
Page 79 of 227 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
electric outboard motors, ranging from 5 hp (more than twice the power of a typical electric trolling motor) to
80 hp, in the Torqeedo's Deep Blue 80 T.
Here it's only responsible to warn those considering buying one: Sit down first. The Deep Blue 80 T costs
$21,999, roughly double the price of an equivalent gas-powered Yamaha F75. And that's before your
whale-research expedition issues the PO for the high-voltage battery: $16,499.
Electric outboards get more cost-competitive as they get smaller. Elco Motor Yachts in upstate New York has
been making electric boats in some form for more than 120 years. Its EP-9.9 electric outboard (brushless,
water-cooled motor, 130 pounds static thrust) retails for $2,910, which is comparable to a gas-fired Yamaha
F9.9.
But what about the batteries? There is still some sticker shock, but in lake country it's not as bad as it seems.
For one thing, jon boats and pontoon boats can hum along at modest speeds all day on four 12-volt car
batteries (48 volts), then return to the dock to charge. But just like an electric car, the faster you go the shorter
the range.
A quartet of deep-cycle, absorbent-glass-mat (AGM) batteries will run about $1,500. It's like buying a lifetime
supply of gas up front.
However, as a consumer proposition, electric outboard motors are not about cost-benefit and only
tangentially about protecting the environment. These are about access.
Mr. Eberlin has sold a few Torqeedo units, all under 10 hp. "There's another bunch of lakes where boats are
limited to 10 horsepower" to encourage compliance with no-wake policies, he said.
One such spot is nearby Innsbrook, founded in 1971, which has 103 lakes dotting its 7,500 acres, the largest
of which is 236 acres. All are no-gas, no-wake. "People get tired of the hustle and bustle of Lake of the
Ozarks," said Chip Wobbe, president of development for Innsbrook. Fast powerboats and jet skis erode
public shoreline and leak fuel and oil into these contained bodies of water.
Strangely, World War II played its part. After the war, thousands of aluminum pontoons and empty fuel tanks
(drop tanks) were scrapped. Poor but enthusiastic boat-builders -- like my people back in North Carolina -- did
what seemed natural: Assemble a floating platform with a canopy overhead. Today, pontoon boats are one of
the pillars of the electric-outboard boat market.
"Pontoon boats with e-drive are a big part of the silent boating culture here," said Mr. Wobbe. He also owns
another example of electric, extra-leisure watercraft: a Duffy. These are purpose-built electric boats with
stem-to-stern canopies. The Duffy has inboard power and uses 6V golf-cart batteries. According to inventor
Marshall Duffield, they are "bay and lake cruisers."
Environmental awareness has also played a part. "Tesla made it easier to imagine," agreed Dean
Heinemann, Elco director of sales. But the bigger driver was the rise of lakes restricted to electric drive only.
Some of these are designated water-supply reservoirs; some are surrounded by homes; others are delicate
habitat under pressure from duck hunters and fishermen. Often all three.
But they also have fish. And where there are fish, there are anglers in a hurry to catch them. The growth in
electric outboards, said Mr. Heinemann, reflects these sportsmen's desire for stern units with a little more
oomph than the traditional trolling motor, which is not designed for sustained, max-speed operation.
Electric-only fishing tournaments are now commonplace, and they are just like their gas-powered
counterparts, except that instead of roaring away at the start, they hum.
But is it really sporting to sneak up on fish that way?

FIAT CHRYSLER
Vehicles Are Recalled
Over Air Bags, Belts
Fiat Chrysler Automobiles NV recalled 1.9 million vehicles world-wide for possible air-bag and seat-belt
failures linked to at least three deaths and five injuries.
The Italian-U.S. auto maker said on Thursday that vehicles equipped with a certain occupant restraint control
module and front-impact sensor wiring with a specific design can fail to activate air bags and seat-belt
restraints in some crashes.
Fiat Chrysler no longer uses the affected parts, the company said. It is "aware of three fatalities and five
injuries that may potentially be related to this condition."
The auto maker said it uncovered the problem analyzing crashes and collecting data from vehicles.
Fiat Chrysler is among auto makers facing unprecedented scrutiny from regulators for safety lapses.
-- Mike Spector
---
BARRICK GOLD
Mine Operations Halt
After Argentine Spill
Barrick Gold Corp. on Thursday said government authorities have temporarily halted operations at its
Veladero gold mine in Argentina to allow for an inspection of the heap leach area after a small amount of
processing solution spilled late last week.
The Toronto-based gold-mining company, the world's largest by output, said a pipe carrying solution used to
extract gold was hit by a block of ice last Thursday, resulting in "a small quantity of solution" spilling off the
leach pad at the mine. It said the solution didn't reach any water sources and the affected area has been
cleaned up.
"The company will work with provincial authorities to confirm the integrity and safety of the heap leach facility
as quickly as possible," Barrick said in a news release. It didn't specify the amount of solution spilled.
-- Judy McKinnon
---
AUTOS
Car Sales Rise in EU;
U.K. Mood Improves
New car sales in the European Union rose 10% in August, with the U.K. market recovering from a Brexit-vote
wobble in June and July, according to data published by the European Automobile Manufacturers'
Association on Thursday.

--- Page 83 ---
Page 83 of 227 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
New car registrations, a mirror for sales, rose to 819,126 vehicles in the EU in August from 744,698 vehicles,
the 36th consecutive month of sales growth. New EU sales in the first eight months of the year rose 8.1% to
9.8 million vehicles, the data show.
U.K. sales rose 3.3% after a near-flat reading in July and an 0.8% fall in June following the vote to quit the

Calm Market Turns Choppy --- Friday's rout may not be the last dip; 'sense that central banks have hit the wall'
Calm Market Turns Choppy --- Friday's rout may not be the last dip; 'sense that central banks have hit
the wall'
By Inyoung Hwang, Paul Vigna and Aaron Kuriloff
1,069 words
12 September 2016
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2016 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Summer vacation is over for markets.
Investors who had bet heavily on calm seas ahead were shaken Friday as prices of stocks, bonds, oil and
gold all slid amid mounting concerns over the willingness and ability of central banks to prop up markets.
Before Friday's rout, in which the S&P 500 fell by 2.5%, the broad stock index hadn't dropped by more than
1% for 52 consecutive trading sessions. Oil and gold prices both had risen more than 20% this year, while
the MSCI Emerging Markets Index had climbed 17% amid confidence the flow of easy money from central
banks would run deeply and indefinitely.
The CBOE Volatility Index, or VIX -- an index considered the market's fear gauge, which tracks investors'
expectations for volatility in stocks -- bounced near multiyear lows during July and August, as traders looked
at tepid economic growth as an excuse for central banks to hold off tightening. On Friday, it jumped 40%.
Stocks fell sharply early Monday in the Asia/Pacific region. Hong Kong's Hang Seng Index opened 2.4%
lower, while Australia's S&P ASX 200 was down nearly 2%. Stocks in Japan, South Korea and Shanghai
were all down more than 1%.
"There's a growing sense that central banks have hit the wall," said David Rosenberg, chief economist and
strategist at Gluskin Sheff & Associates Inc., a Toronto money manager. "The bottom line is there's more
downside risk than upside potential for this market over the next few months."
While some investors this summer warned about complacency and questioned how far assets could rally
amid mixed economic data, many were loath to sit on the sidelines in a rising market. Money went into
everything from small-cap stocks and negative-yielding corporate bonds to emerging-market assets. In the
week before Friday, for example, emerging-market stock funds took in $1.9 billion, according to Michael
Hartnett, chief investment strategist at Bank of America/Merrill Lynch Global Research, sealing a 10th
consecutive week of inflows.
Friday's retreat comes at a time when markets are broadly perceived to be vulnerable to a reversal, thanks to
factors including above-average valuations, uneven global growth and soft U.S. corporate earnings and
revenue. The S&P 500 and Dow Jones Industrial Average had hit fresh records during the summer as equity
funds attracted money flows in four of the past five weeks, Bank of America data show. Investors had also
favored corporate credit and commodity funds over Treasurys and bond proxies such as utility stocks.
A sharp rally in global government-bond prices also has exposed buyers of these traditional havens to greater
volatility.
"Because volatility has been so calm, people started to add risk everywhere," said Rebecca Cheong, head of
Americas equity derivatives strategy at UBS Group AG. "Now things are at extreme levels, even higher than"
August 2015, when China devalued its currency and set shock waves across financial markets and the VIX
jumped 46%.
Speculators who bet heavily that the tranquility would continue paid for it on Friday. Hedge funds and other
large speculators were net short VIX futures by about a record 134,000 contracts as of Sept. 6, according to
Commodity Futures Trading Commission data, a position that reflects an expectation of continued low
volatility. Traders also made big bets on the long side, with bullish wagers on futures tracking the S&P 500,
Nasdaq-100 Index and Russell 2000 index near their highest since 2013 as of Tuesday, according to the
latest data available.
Short covering from the bets against VIX futures could total $153 million over the next few days, according to
UBS's Ms. Cheong. The unwinding of bets would be driven partly by volatility-tracking exchange-traded

--- Page 90 ---
Page 90 of 227 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
securities that would need to rebalance immediately, while hedge funds that were short VIX futures could
face margin calls because of the sudden jump in the gauge, she said.
When investors get margin calls, they often need to sell more assets to post the additional collateral that is
demanded by brokers, moves that further pressure prices.
While Friday represents just one trading day, the turbulence has reignited fears that the Federal Reserve is
moving further into a rate-raising phase that could leave stocks, emerging markets and commodities
vulnerable to a deeper pullback.
Whether Friday's move is a blip or something bigger could hinge on what the Fed does over the next two
weeks.
Three Fed speakers are scheduled to speak Monday, after which the central bank enters a self-imposed
blackout period before its Sept. 20-21 meeting. Federal-funds futures, which are used by traders to place bets
on central-bank policy, on Friday showed 24% odds of a U.S. interest-rate rise in September, compared with
an 18% chance as of Thursday, according to CME Group Inc.
Faith in central banks started to wane last week after the European Central Bank refrained from new policy
moves and signs emerged that the Bank of Japan may run out of available government debt to buy.
And in a speech Friday, Boston Fed President Eric Rosengren, who has long been viewed as one of the
board's most dovish members, said: "A reasonable case can be made for continuing to pursue a gradual
normalization of monetary policy."
Still, some investors said Friday's moves don't necessarily mark an inflection point.
"It's been very quiet in the markets, suddenly you get a bit of noise and people tend to overreact," said Brian
Jacobsen, chief portfolio strategist at Wells Fargo Funds Management. "Sometimes these moves in the
markets can be nasty and brutish, but short."
For example, stocks tumbled after the U.K.'s vote to leave the European Union in June, then rebounded to
fresh highs.
Yet there is scope for the increase in volatility to continue, data suggest. Even after Friday's jump to 17.5, the
VIX remains well below its 2016 high from February of 28. In bond markets, while the benchmark 10-year
Treasury yield jumped to 1.67%on Friday, it is still under the 2.27% level it ended at last year.
---
Corrie Driebusch, Ira Iosebashvili and Kenan Machado contributed to this article.
